      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">
        Dark matter
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">
        dark energy
       </a>
       are the current leading topics in astronomy,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as their discovery and controversy originated during the study of the galaxies.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_subfields">
        Specific subfields
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Specific subfields">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_astronomy">
        Solar astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Solar astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uvsun_trace_big.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Uvsun_trace_big.jpg/220px-Uvsun_trace_big.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Uvsun_trace_big.jpg/330px-Uvsun_trace_big.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Uvsun_trace_big.jpg/440px-Uvsun_trace_big.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uvsun_trace_big.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An
         <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
          ultraviolet
         </a>
         image of the Sun's active
         <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">
          photosphere
         </a>
         as viewed by the
         <a href="/wiki/TRACE" title="TRACE">
          TRACE
         </a>
         space telescope.
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
           NASA
          </a>
          photo
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg/220px-Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg/330px-Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg/440px-Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Observat%C3%B3rium_Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt_1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Solar observatory
         <a href="/wiki/Lomnick%C3%BD_%C5%A1t%C3%ADt" title="Lomnický štít">
          Lomnický štít
         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">
          Slovakia
         </a>
         ) built in 1962
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_telescope" title="Solar telescope">
        Solar telescope
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       At a distance of about eight light-minutes, the most frequently studied star is the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       , a typical main-sequence
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_star" title="Dwarf star">
        dwarf star
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_class" title="Stellar class">
        stellar class
       </a>
       G2 V, and about 4.6 billion years (Gyr) old. The Sun is not considered a
       <a href="/wiki/Variable_star" title="Variable star">
        variable star
       </a>
       , but it does undergo periodic changes in activity known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspot_cycle" title="Sunspot cycle">
        sunspot cycle
       </a>
       . This is an 11-year oscillation in
       <a href="/wiki/Wolf_number" title="Wolf number">
        sunspot number
       </a>
       . Sunspots are regions of lower-than- average temperatures that are associated with intense magnetic activity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_FAQ_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-solar_FAQ-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Sun has steadily increased in luminosity by 40% since it first became a main-sequence star. The Sun has also undergone periodic changes in luminosity that can have a significant impact on the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Environmental_issues_:_essential_primary_sources._66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Environmental_issues_:_essential_primary_sources.-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maunder_minimum" title="Maunder minimum">
        Maunder minimum
       </a>
       , for example, is believed to have caused the
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">
        Little Ice Age
       </a>
       phenomenon during the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-future-sun_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-future-sun-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The visible outer surface of the Sun is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">
        photosphere
       </a>
       . Above this layer is a thin region known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Chromosphere" title="Chromosphere">
        chromosphere
       </a>
       . This is surrounded by a transition region of rapidly increasing temperatures, and finally by the super-heated
       <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">
        corona
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       At the center of the Sun is the core region, a volume of sufficient temperature and pressure for
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
        nuclear fusion
       </a>
       to occur. Above the core is the
       <a href="/wiki/Radiation_zone" title="Radiation zone">
        radiation zone
       </a>
       , where the plasma conveys the energy flux by means of radiation. Above that is the
       <a href="/wiki/Convection_zone" title="Convection zone">
        convection zone
       </a>
       where the gas material transports energy primarily through physical displacement of the gas known as convection. It is believed that the movement of mass within the convection zone creates the magnetic activity that generates sunspots.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solar_FAQ_65-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-solar_FAQ-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A solar wind of plasma particles constantly streams outward from the Sun until, at the outermost limit of the Solar System, it reaches the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">
        heliopause
       </a>
       . As the solar wind passes the Earth, it interacts with the
       <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">
        Earth's magnetic field
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       ) and deflects the solar wind, but traps some creating the
       <a href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belt" title="Van Allen radiation belt">
        Van Allen radiation belts
       </a>
       that envelop the Earth . The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">
        aurora
       </a>
       are created when solar wind particles are guided by the magnetic flux lines into the Earth's polar regions where the lines the descend into the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_science">
        Planetary science
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Planetary science">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">
        Planetary science
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_geology" title="Planetary geology">
        Planetary geology
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Planetary science is the study of the assemblage of
       <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
        planets
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        moons
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
        dwarf planets
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
        comets
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       , and other bodies orbiting the Sun, as well as extrasolar planets. The
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       has been relatively well-studied, initially through telescopes and then later by spacecraft. This has provided a good overall understanding of the formation and evolution of this planetary system, although many new discoveries are still being made.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geology_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-geology-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="750" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg/220px-Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg/330px-Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg/440px-Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dust.devil.mars.arp.750pix.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The black spot at the top is a
         <a href="/wiki/Dust_devil" title="Dust devil">
          dust devil
         </a>
         climbing a crater wall on
         <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
          Mars
         </a>
         . This moving, swirling column of
         <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">
          Martian atmosphere
         </a>
         (comparable to a terrestrial
         <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">
          tornado
         </a>
         ) created the long, dark streak.
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
           NASA
          </a>
          image
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Solar System is subdivided into the inner planets, the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        asteroid belt
       </a>
       , and the outer planets. The inner
       <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">
        terrestrial planets
       </a>
       consist of
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       . The outer
       <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">
        gas giant
       </a>
       planets are
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
        Uranus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
        Neptune
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planets_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-planets-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Beyond Neptune lies the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuiper_Belt" title="Kuiper Belt">
        Kuiper Belt
       </a>
       , and finally the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oort_Cloud" title="Oort Cloud">
        Oort Cloud
       </a>
       , which may extend as far as a light-year.
      </p>
      <p>
       The planets were formed 4.6 billion years ago in the
       <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">
        protoplanetary disk
       </a>
       that surrounded the early Sun. Through a process that included gravitational attraction, collision, and accretion, the disk formed clumps of matter that, with time, became protoplanets. The
       <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">
        radiation pressure
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       then expelled most of the unaccreted matter, and only those planets with sufficient mass retained their gaseous atmosphere. The planets continued to sweep up, or eject, the remaining matter during a period of intense bombardment, evidenced by the many
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact craters
       </a>
       on the Moon. During this period, some of the protoplanets may have collided and one such collision may have
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">
        formed the Moon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montmerle2006_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Montmerle2006-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Once a planet reaches sufficient mass, the materials of different densities segregate within, during
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        planetary differentiation
       </a>
       . This process can form a stony or metallic core, surrounded by a mantle and an outer crust. The core may include solid and liquid regions, and some planetary cores generate their own
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic field
       </a>
       , which can protect their atmospheres from solar wind stripping.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A planet or moon's interior heat is produced from the collisions that created the body, by the decay of radioactive materials (
       <i>
        e.g.
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
        uranium
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">
        thorium
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Aluminium#Isotopes" title="Aluminium">
        <sup>
         26
        </sup>
        Al
       </a>
       ), or
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">
        tidal heating
       </a>
       caused by interactions with other bodies. Some planets and moons accumulate enough heat to drive geologic processes such as
       <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">
        volcanism
       </a>
       and tectonics. Those that accumulate or retain an
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       can also undergo surface
       <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">
        erosion
       </a>
       from wind or water. Smaller bodies, without tidal heating, cool more quickly; and their geological activity ceases with the exception of impact cratering.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_solar_system_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-new_solar_system-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stellar_astronomy">
        Stellar astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Stellar astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ant_Nebula.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="1072" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ant_Nebula.jpg/220px-Ant_Nebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ant_Nebula.jpg/330px-Ant_Nebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ant_Nebula.jpg/440px-Ant_Nebula.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ant_Nebula.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mz3" title="Mz3">
          Ant planetary nebula
         </a>
         . Ejecting gas from the dying central star shows symmetrical patterns unlike the chaotic patterns of ordinary explosions.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        Star
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The study of stars and
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">
        stellar evolution
       </a>
       is fundamental to our understanding of the Universe. The astrophysics of stars has been determined through observation and theoretical understanding; and from computer simulations of the interior.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amos7_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Amos7-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">
        Star formation
       </a>
       occurs in dense regions of dust and gas, known as
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_nebula" title="Dark nebula">
        giant molecular clouds
       </a>
       . When destabilized, cloud fragments can collapse under the influence of gravity, to form a
       <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">
        protostar
       </a>
       . A sufficiently dense, and hot, core region will trigger
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
        nuclear fusion
       </a>
       , thus creating a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-sequence_star" title="Main-sequence star">
        main-sequence star
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2004_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith2004-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Almost all elements heavier than
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        hydrogen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        helium
       </a>
       were
       <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">
        created
       </a>
       inside the cores of stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amos7_74-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Amos7-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The characteristics of the resulting star depend primarily upon its starting mass. The more massive the star, the greater its luminosity, and the more rapidly it fuses its hydrogen fuel into helium in its core. Over time, this hydrogen fuel is completely converted into helium, and the star begins to
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">
        evolve
       </a>
       . The fusion of helium requires a higher core temperature. A star with a high enough core temperature will push its outer layers outward while increasing its core density. The resulting
       <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">
        red giant
       </a>
       formed by the expanding outer layers enjoys a brief life span, before the helium fuel in the core is in turn consumed. Very massive stars can also undergo a series of evolutionary phases, as they fuse increasingly heavier elements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amos_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Amos-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The final fate of the star depends on its mass, with stars of mass greater than about eight times the Sun becoming core collapse
       <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">
        supernovae
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while smaller stars blow off their outer layers and leave behind the inert core in the form of a
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarf
       </a>
       . The ejection of the outer layers forms a
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">
        planetary nebula
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The remnant of a supernova is a dense
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron star
       </a>
       , or, if the stellar mass was at least three times that of the Sun, a
       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">
        black hole
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_atlas_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_atlas-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Closely orbiting binary stars can follow more complex evolutionary paths, such as mass transfer onto a white dwarf companion that can potentially cause a supernova.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Planetary nebulae and supernovae distribute the "
       <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">
        metals
       </a>
       " produced in the star by fusion to the interstellar medium; without them, all new stars (and their planetary systems) would be formed from hydrogen and helium alone.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_astronomy" title="Solar astronomy">
        Solar astronomy
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_astronomy">
        Galactic astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Galactic astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="518" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg/220px-Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg/330px-Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg/440px-Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Observed structure of the
         <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
          Milky Way
         </a>
         's spiral arms
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Galactic_astronomy" title="Galactic astronomy">
        Galactic astronomy
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Our
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">
        solar system
       </a>
       orbits within the
       <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
        Milky Way
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">
        barred spiral galaxy
       </a>
       that is a prominent member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">
        Local Group
       </a>
       of galaxies. It is a rotating mass of gas, dust, stars and other objects, held together by mutual gravitational attraction. As the Earth is located within the dusty outer arms, there are large portions of the Milky Way that are obscured from view.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the center of the Milky Way is the core, a bar-shaped bulge with what is believed to be a
       <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">
        supermassive black hole
       </a>
       at its center. This is surrounded by four primary arms that spiral from the core. This is a region of active star formation that contains many younger,
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_population" title="Stellar population">
        population I
       </a>
       stars. The disk is surrounded by a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_spheroid" title="Galactic spheroid">
        spheroid halo
       </a>
       of older,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_II" title="Population II">
        population II
       </a>
       stars, as well as relatively dense concentrations of stars known as
       <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">
        globular clusters
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between the stars lies the
       <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">
        interstellar medium
       </a>
       , a region of sparse matter. In the densest regions,
       <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">
        molecular clouds
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        molecular hydrogen
       </a>
       and other elements create star-forming regions. These begin as a compact
       <a href="/wiki/Pre-stellar_core" title="Pre-stellar core">
        pre-stellar core
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_nebula" title="Dark nebula">
        dark nebulae
       </a>
       , which concentrate and collapse (in volumes determined by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeans_length" title="Jeans length">
        Jeans length
       </a>
       ) to form compact protostars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2004_75-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith2004-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As the more massive stars appear, they transform the cloud into an
       <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">
        H II region
       </a>
       (ionized atomic hydrogen) of glowing gas and plasma. The
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        stellar wind
       </a>
       and supernova explosions from these stars eventually cause the cloud to disperse, often leaving behind one or more young
       <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">
        open clusters
       </a>
       of stars. These clusters gradually disperse, and the stars join the population of the Milky Way.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Kinematic studies of matter in the Milky Way and other galaxies have demonstrated that there is more mass than can be accounted for by visible matter. A
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_halo" title="Dark matter halo">
        dark matter halo
       </a>
       appears to dominate the mass, although the nature of this dark matter remains undetermined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extragalactic_astronomy">
        Extragalactic astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Extragalactic astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2022" data-file-width="2022" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg/220px-Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg/330px-Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg/440px-Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grav.lens1.arp.750pix.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         This image shows several blue, loop-shaped objects that are multiple images of the same galaxy, duplicated by the
         <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">
          gravitational lens
         </a>
         effect of the cluster of yellow galaxies near the middle of the photograph. The lens is produced by the cluster's gravitational field that bends light to magnify and distort the image of a more distant object.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Extragalactic_astronomy" title="Extragalactic astronomy">
        Extragalactic astronomy
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The study of objects outside our galaxy is a branch of astronomy concerned with the
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">
        formation and evolution of Galaxies
       </a>
       , their morphology (description) and
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification" title="Galaxy morphological classification">
        classification
       </a>
       , the observation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_galaxy" title="Active galaxy">
        active galaxies
       </a>
       , and at a larger scale, the
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">
        groups and clusters of galaxies
       </a>
       . Finally, the latter is important for the understanding of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">
        large-scale structure of the cosmos
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Most
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">
        galaxies
       </a>
       are organized into distinct shapes that allow for classification schemes. They are commonly divided into
       <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">
        spiral
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">
        elliptical
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Irregular_galaxy" title="Irregular galaxy">
        Irregular
       </a>
       galaxies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As the name suggests, an elliptical galaxy has the cross-sectional shape of an
       <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">
        ellipse
       </a>
       . The stars move along
       <a href="/wiki/Randomness" title="Randomness">
        random
       </a>
       orbits with no preferred direction. These galaxies contain little or no interstellar dust, few star-forming regions, and generally older stars. Elliptical galaxies are more commonly found at the core of galactic clusters, and may have been formed through mergers of large galaxies.
      </p>
      <p>
       A spiral galaxy is organized into a flat, rotating disk, usually with a prominent bulge or bar at the center, and trailing bright arms that spiral outward. The arms are dusty regions of star formation within which massive young stars produce a blue tint. Spiral galaxies are typically surrounded by a halo of older stars. Both the
       <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
        Milky Way
       </a>
       and one of our nearest galaxy neighbors, the
       <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">
        Andromeda Galaxy
       </a>
       , are spiral galaxies.
      </p>
      <p>
       Irregular galaxies are chaotic in appearance, and are neither spiral nor elliptical. About a quarter of all galaxies are irregular, and the peculiar shapes of such galaxies may be the result of gravitational interaction.
      </p>
      <p>
       An active galaxy is a formation that emits a significant amount of its energy from a source other than its stars, dust and gas. It is powered by a compact region at the core, thought to be a super-massive black hole that is emitting radiation from in-falling material.
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_galaxy" title="Radio galaxy">
        radio galaxy
       </a>
       is an active galaxy that is very luminous in the
       <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">
        radio
       </a>
       portion of the spectrum, and is emitting immense plumes or lobes of gas. Active galaxies that emit shorter frequency, high-energy radiation include
       <a href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxy" title="Seyfert galaxy">
        Seyfert galaxies
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">
        Quasars
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">
        Blazars
       </a>
       . Quasars are believed to be the most consistently luminous objects in the known universe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">
        large-scale structure of the cosmos
       </a>
       is represented by groups and clusters of galaxies. This structure is organized into a hierarchy of groupings, with the largest being the
       <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">
        superclusters
       </a>
       . The collective matter is formed into
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">
        filaments
       </a>
       and walls, leaving large
       <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">
        voids
       </a>
       between.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolving_universe_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-evolving_universe-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">
        Physical cosmology
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">
        Cosmology
       </a>
       (from the Greek κόσμος (
       <i>
        kosmos
       </i>
       ) "world, universe" and λόγος (
       <i>
        logos
       </i>
       ) "word, study" or literally "logic") could be considered the study of the Universe as a whole.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_(full_resolution).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2078" data-file-width="2382" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/250px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/375px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/500px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_(full_resolution).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">
          Hubble Extreme Deep Field
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Observations of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_Universe" title="Large-scale structure of the Universe">
        large-scale structure of the Universe
       </a>
       , a branch known as
       <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">
        physical cosmology
       </a>
       , have provided a deep understanding of the formation and evolution of the cosmos. Fundamental to modern cosmology is the well-accepted theory of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang" title="Big bang">
        big bang
       </a>
       , wherein our Universe began at a single point in time, and thereafter
       <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">
        expanded
       </a>
       over the course of 13.8 billion years
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to its present condition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodelson2003_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dodelson2003-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The concept of the big bang can be traced back to the discovery of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">
        microwave background radiation
       </a>
       in 1965.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodelson2003_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dodelson2003-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the course of this expansion, the Universe underwent several evolutionary stages. In the very early moments, it is theorized that the Universe experienced a very rapid
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">
        cosmic inflation
       </a>
       , which homogenized the starting conditions. Thereafter,
       <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">
        nucleosynthesis
       </a>
       produced the elemental abundance of the early Universe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodelson2003_89-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dodelson2003-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (See also
       <a href="/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology" title="Nucleocosmochronology">
        nucleocosmochronology
       </a>
       .)
      </p>
      <p>
       When the first neutral
       <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">
        atoms
       </a>
       formed from a sea of primordial ions, space became transparent to radiation, releasing the energy viewed today as the microwave background radiation. The expanding Universe then underwent a Dark Age due to the lack of stellar energy sources.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmology_101_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cosmology_101-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A hierarchical structure of matter began to form from minute variations in the mass density of space. Matter accumulated in the densest regions, forming clouds of gas and the earliest stars, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_III_stars" title="Population III stars">
        Population III stars
       </a>
       . These massive stars triggered the
       <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">
        reionization
       </a>
       process and are believed to have created many of the heavy elements in the early Universe, which, through nuclear decay, create lighter elements, allowing the cycle of nucleosynthesis to continue longer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Gravitational aggregations clustered into filaments, leaving voids in the gaps. Gradually, organizations of gas and dust merged to form the first primitive galaxies. Over time, these pulled in more matter, and were often organized into
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">
        groups and clusters
       </a>
       of galaxies, then into larger-scale superclusters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Fundamental to the structure of the Universe is the existence of
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">
        dark matter
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">
        dark energy
       </a>
       . These are now thought to be its dominant components, forming 96% of the mass of the Universe. For this reason, much effort is expended in trying to understand the physics of these components.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Interdisciplinary_studies">
        Interdisciplinary studies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Interdisciplinary studies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Astronomy and astrophysics have developed significant interdisciplinary links with other major scientific fields.
       <a href="/wiki/Archaeoastronomy" title="Archaeoastronomy">
        Archaeoastronomy
       </a>
       is the study of ancient or traditional astronomies in their cultural context, utilizing
       <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">
        archaeological
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">
        anthropological
       </a>
       evidence.
       <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">
        Astrobiology
       </a>
       is the study of the advent and evolution of biological systems in the Universe, with particular emphasis on the possibility of non-terrestrial life.
       <a href="/wiki/Astrostatistics" title="Astrostatistics">
        Astrostatistics
       </a>
       is the application of statistics to astrophysics to the analysis of vast amount of observational astrophysical data.
      </p>
      <p>
       The study of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">
        chemicals
       </a>
       found in space, including their formation, interaction and destruction, is called
       <a href="/wiki/Astrochemistry" title="Astrochemistry">
        astrochemistry
       </a>
       . These substances are usually found in
       <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">
        molecular clouds
       </a>
       , although they may also appear in low temperature stars, brown dwarfs and planets.
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmochemistry" title="Cosmochemistry">
        Cosmochemistry
       </a>
       is the study of the chemicals found within the Solar System, including the origins of the elements and variations in the
       <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">
        isotope
       </a>
       ratios. Both of these fields represent an overlap of the disciplines of astronomy and chemistry. As "
       <a href="/wiki/Forensic_astronomy" title="Forensic astronomy">
        forensic astronomy
       </a>
       ", finally, methods from astronomy have been used to solve problems of law and history.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Amateur_astronomy">
        Amateur astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Amateur astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomy" title="Amateur astronomy">
        Amateur astronomy
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Telescope_trailer_22.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="540" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Telescope_trailer_22.jpg/170px-Telescope_trailer_22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Telescope_trailer_22.jpg/255px-Telescope_trailer_22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Telescope_trailer_22.jpg/340px-Telescope_trailer_22.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Telescope_trailer_22.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Amateur astronomers can build their own equipment, and hold star parties and gatherings, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Stellafane" title="Stellafane">
          Stellafane
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Astronomy is one of the sciences to which amateurs can contribute the most.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Collectively, amateur astronomers observe a variety of celestial objects and phenomena sometimes with
       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_telescope_making" title="Amateur telescope making">
        equipment that they build themselves
       </a>
       . Common targets of amateur astronomers include the Sun, the Moon, planets, stars, comets,
       <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">
        meteor showers
       </a>
       , and a variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Deep-sky_object" title="Deep-sky object">
        deep-sky objects
       </a>
       such as star clusters, galaxies, and nebulae. Astronomy clubs are located throughout the world and many have programs to help their members set up and complete observational programs including those to observe all the objects in the Messier (110 objects) or Herschel 400 catalogues of points of interest in the night sky. One branch of amateur astronomy, amateur
       <a href="/wiki/Astrophotography" title="Astrophotography">
        astrophotography
       </a>
       , involves the taking of photos of the night sky. Many amateurs like to specialize in the observation of particular objects, types of objects, or types of events which interest them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most amateurs work at visible wavelengths, but a small minority experiment with wavelengths outside the visible spectrum. This includes the use of infrared filters on conventional telescopes, and also the use of radio telescopes. The pioneer of amateur radio astronomy was
       <a href="/wiki/Karl_Guthe_Jansky" title="Karl Guthe Jansky">
        Karl Jansky
       </a>
       , who started observing the sky at radio wavelengths in the 1930s. A number of amateur astronomers use either homemade telescopes or use radio telescopes which were originally built for astronomy research but which are now available to amateurs (
       <i>
        e.g.
       </i>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/One-Mile_Telescope" title="One-Mile Telescope">
        One-Mile Telescope
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Amateur astronomers continue to make scientific contributions to the field of astronomy and it is one of the few scientific disciplines where amateurs can still make significant contributions. Amateurs can make occultation measurements that are used to refine the orbits of minor planets. They can also discover comets, and perform regular observations of variable stars. Improvements in digital technology have allowed amateurs to make impressive advances in the field of astrophotography.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Unsolved_problems_in_astronomy">
        Unsolved problems in astronomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Astronomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Unsolved problems in astronomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">
        List of unsolved problems in physics
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although the scientific discipline of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_astronomy" title="Unsolved problems in astronomy">
        astronomy
       </a>
       has made tremendous strides in understanding the nature of the Universe and its contents, there remain some important unanswered questions. Answers to these may require the construction of new ground- and space-based instruments, and possibly new developments in theoretical and experimental physics.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        What is the origin of the stellar mass spectrum? That is, why do astronomers observe the same distribution of stellar masses – the
        <a href="/wiki/Initial_mass_function" title="Initial mass function">
         initial mass function
        </a>
        – apparently regardless of the initial conditions?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
         <a href="#cite_note-102">
          [102]
         </a>
        </sup>
        A deeper understanding of the formation of stars and planets is needed.
       </li>
       <li>
        Is there other
        <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">
         life in the Universe
        </a>
        ? Especially, is there other intelligent life? If so, what is the explanation for the
        <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox" title="Fermi paradox">
         Fermi paradox
        </a>
        ? The existence of life elsewhere has important scientific and philosophical implications.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
         <a href="#cite_note-103">
          [103]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
         <a href="#cite_note-104">
          [104]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Is the Solar System normal or atypical?
       </li>
       <li>
        What is the nature of
        <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">
         dark matter
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">
         dark energy
        </a>
        ? These dominate the evolution and fate of the cosmos, yet their true nature remains unknown.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physics_questions_105-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-physics_questions-105">
          [105]
         </a>
        </sup>
        What will be the
        <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe" title="Ultimate fate of the universe">
         ultimate fate of the universe
        </a>
        ?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
         <a href="#cite_note-106">
          [106]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        How did the first galaxies form?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
         <a href="#cite_note-107">
          [107]
         </a>
        </sup>
        How did supermassive black holes form?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
         <a href="#cite_note-108">
          [108]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        What is creating the
        <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high-energy_cosmic_ray" title="Ultra-high-energy cosmic ray">
         ultra-high-energy cosmic rays
        </a>
        ?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
         <a href="#cite_note-109">
          [109]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Why is the abundance of lithium in the cosmos four times lower than predicted by the standard
        <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">
         Big Bang
        </a>
        model?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
         <a href="#cite_note-110">
          [110]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        What really happens beyond the
        <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">
         event horizon
        </a>
        ?
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
         <a href="#cite_note-111">
          [111]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>

           Stellar Evolution
          </i>
         </a>
         . A K Peters, Ltd.
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
          ISBN
         </a>

          978-1-56881-012-6
         </a>
         .
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Stellar+Evolution&amp;rft.pub=A+K+Peters%2C+Ltd&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-56881-012-6&amp;rft.aulast=Harpaz&amp;rft.aufirst=Amos&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3Dkd4VEZv8oo0C%26dq&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAstronomy">
         <span style="display:none;">
